    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="fa1e153d31f54d56b1d37025f8a60da1">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'f89e5f04-c749-4347-b9cf-358167e2b972'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d95533114" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Transition metal sulfide-based anodes usually suffer&nbsp;from huge volumetric change and sluggish reaction kinetics, hindering their application for long-term and high-power/energy sodium-ion batteries. Herein, a new design of CoS<sub>2</sub><sub>-</sub><sub>x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub> (0≤x≤2) nanocrystals with highly controllable selenium substitution and S, Se-codoped graphene immobilization (CoS<sub>2</sub><sub>-</sub><sub>x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG) is proposed to tune the reaction kinetics and structural stability. The nanocrystal-on-graphene structure and robust C─S &amp;C─Se bonding rivets between CoS<sub>2</sub><sub>-</sub><sub>x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub> and SG greatly improve the structural stability of the CoS<sub>2</sub><sub>-</sub><sub>x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG. Electrochemical performance, kinetic analysis, and theoretical calculation reveal that Se substitution plays a double-edged role in sodium storage: the increase of Se substitution content enhances the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics but decreases the Na-storage capacity. When the Se substitution content is 0.4, the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG electrode demonstrates the best performance: high initial Coulombic efficiency (95.5%), ultrahigh rate capability (412.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 30&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and ultra-stable cycling performance (97.6% capacity retention after 1000 cycles). In situ/ex situ measurements further unveil that the conversion reaction between Co<sup>0</sup> and Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se generates the micro-scaled CoSe<sub>2</sub>–CoS<sub>2</sub> heterostructure, synergistically improving the Na-storage active sites and reaction kinetics. This work provides a controllable anion substitution strategy to balance the Na<sup>+</sup> storage active sites and kinetics with potential applications for high-power/energy sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202310256-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202310256-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered a feasible candidate for large-scale energy storage due to their merits of abundant sodium resources in Earth's crust.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0001_R_d95533106e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Rev.">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0004_R_d95533106e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the larger radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02&nbsp;Å) compared to Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76&nbsp;Å) results in poor Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics and significant volume change upon sodiation/desodiation, making many mature lithium-ion batteries (LIBs)&nbsp;techniques and materials challenging or infeasible when used in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0005_R_d95533106e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Commun.">5</a>, <a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0006_R_d95533106e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, the commercial graphite anode in LIBs is not applicable in SIBs due to its poor Na-storage performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0007_R_d95533106e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Commun.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Searching for suitable electrode materials with high reversible capacity and fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics is vitally important. Recently, transition metal sulfides (TMSs) have been extensively studied for SIBs anode due to their high theoretical specific capacity and decent electrochemical activity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0008_R_d95533106e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0009_R_d95533106e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the intrinsic mediocre conductivity usually leads to poor electrochemical reversibility and slow Na<sup>+</sup> reaction kinetics. Moreover, the huge volume expansion/contraction based on the conversion mechanism usually induces severe structural collapse, breakdown/regrowth of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI), and electric disconnection from the current collectors.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0010_R_d95533106e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To circumvent the above issues, an ideal construction of electrode materials requires the consideration of all essential components, including particles of active materials, electronic/ionic conductivity, and electrode/electrolyte compatibility/stability at the cell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0011_R_d95533106e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the bulk design of particles, downsizing the TMSs to the nanoscale can enhance the reaction kinetics by shortening the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion lengths and increasing the Na<sup>+</sup> storage active sites. Moreover, the nanosized TMSs could self-buffer the inner stress upon the sodiation/desodiation and alleviate the repeated SEI fracture/regrowth. However, nanosized nanoparticles generally suffer from severe particle aggregation triggered by high specific surface energy. Introducing conductive carbon to the TMSs could improve the electrical conductivity of TMSs and buffer the volume variation upon the cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0012_R_d95533106e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Commun.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0013_R_d95533106e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nonetheless, the weak bonding strength between polar TMSs and nonpolar carbon cannot fix and capture the TMSs effectively, which leads to material detaching from the carbon matrix and impedes the charge-transfer upon long-term cycling.</p>
         
         <p>A further step to fully unlock the power of TMS electrodes is electronic structure engineering.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0006_R_d95533106e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this regard, the anion doping/substitution strategy is an effective approach to tune the electronic structure of TMSs and potentially improve the associated electrochemical activity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0014_R_d95533106e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nat. Commun.">14</a>-<a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0017_R_d95533106e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Typically, incorporating selenium (Se) into the TMSs could positively alter the physicochemical properties of the TMSs with multi-functionality.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0018_R_d95533106e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0019_R_d95533106e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Z. Sun, Energy Storage Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> First, the Se has a larger atomic radius (1.98&nbsp;Å vs 1.84&nbsp;Å) and enhanced shielding effect than S, enabling the valence electron of the Se atom with less constraint from the nuclear charge.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0020_R_d95533106e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chem. Eng. J.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, the Se doping/substitution could narrow the band gaps and enhance the electronic conductivity of TMSs. Second, the bond energy of metal–Se is weaker than that of metal–S bond, resulting in enhanced redox kinetics during the conversion reactions. For example, Huang et&nbsp;al. reported the few-layered MoSSe nanoribbons for SIBs, which deliver a high capacity of 361.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 6.4&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 90% capacity retention after 600 cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0021_R_d95533106e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hu et&nbsp;al. developed a SnSe<sub>0.5</sub>S<sub>0.5</sub>/graphene nanostructure with interface and structure engineering, showing a high rate performance of 387&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 10&nbsp;A g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0018_R_d95533106e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although these emerging reports, some challenges and inadequacies still remain based on the Se doped/substituted TMS materials: 1) In most cases, the large volume change and weak bonding strength between polar TMSs and nonpolar carbon make TMSs difficult to achieve a long cycle life (&gt;1000 cycles) with high capacity retention (&gt;95%). 2) The single-phase TMS with controllable Se substitution content is difficult to implement and rarely reported for SIBs, especially for cobalt sulfide-based electrodes. 3) The underlying relationship and mechanism between Se doping/substitution content and electrochemical performance have not been disclosed yet. It is desirable to unveil the balanced electrochemical performance between the Na-storage active sites and reaction kinetics. 4) As typical conversion-type materials, TMSs will experience significant phase changes with bond breaking/formation during sodiation/desodiation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0022_R_d95533106e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Especially for the heteroatom doping/substitution systems, the complicated phase transitions may cause disintegration and rearrangement of original crystal structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0017_R_d95533106e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is highly desired to unveil the structural evolution of the Se-doped/substituted TMSs for a better understanding of the fundamental mechanism.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, for the first time, we controllably synthesize a series of Se substituted CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub> (0≤x≤2) nanocrystals anchoring on S, Se-codoped graphene (SG) nanosheets through a synchronous sulfurization/selenization strategy. The CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub> (0≤x≤2) nanocrystals (&lt;50&nbsp;nm) strongly anchor on the SG nanosheets through C─S─Co &amp;C─Se─Co bonds, which not only ensures shortened Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion length and sufficient sodium insertion but also affords fast electron transfer and robust structure. Thus, the CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub> nanocrystal on graphene experiences a “poreforming−repairing” phenomenon, which avoids the nanocrystal “self-fracture” and alleviates the SEI film breakdown/regrowth. Experimental results and theoretical calculation reveal that the Se substitution can improve the electronic conductivity of the CoS<sub>2</sub> and promote the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. While excess Se substitution reduces the Na-storage active sites, leading to a lower specific capacity. As a consequence, the CoS<sub>2</sub> with an optimal Se substitution <i>x</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;0.4 (CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG) delivers the best sodium storage performance: high initial Coulombic efficiency (95.5%), ultrahigh rate capability (412.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 30&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and ultra-stable cycling performance (97.6% capacity retention after 1000 cycles). To the best of our knowledge, the sodium storage performances of CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG are the best among all the published works based on CoS<sub>2</sub>, demonstrating that the smart nano/micro-scale design is highly appealing for advanced electrode application. Further, when the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG anode is coupled with the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C cathode, the full cell delivers excellent rate capability and long-term cycling performance.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202310256-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202310256-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The synthesis process of CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG composites (<i>x</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;0, 0.4, 1.0, 1.6, and 2) is schematically shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0001">1a</a>. First, the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> growing on reduced graphene oxide (Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@rGO) was prepared via a facile solvothermal method (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). After annealing in Ar at 600&nbsp;°C, the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@rGO was converted to Co─CoO@rGO (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Finally, the Se substituted CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG was obtained through a synchronous selenization/sulfurization of the Co─CoO@rGO precursor by using the sulfur/selenium mixture. The reaction between sulfur/selenium and Co─CoO@rGO should be ascribed to the following reaction processes:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0023_R_d95533106e652" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0024_R_d95533106e652" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Proc. Electrochem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202310256-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/8e6c48c4-fa97-4da6-afe9-f225c609c3e7/adfm202310256-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202310256:adfm202310256-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202310256-math-0001.png">

                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}yS + zSe \to {S_y}S{e_z}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202310256-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/79cbe817-ef08-4ce5-8b38-0bcaa0921a0f/adfm202310256-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202310256:adfm202310256-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202310256-math-0002.png">

                           <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                              <mi>unbalanced</mi>
                           </mfenced>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{Co}} + {S_y}S{e_z} \to Co{S_{2 - x}}S{e_x}\left( {{\mathrm{unbalanced}}} \right)\end{equation}$$</annotation>

                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}CoO + {S_y}S{e_z} \to Co{S_{2 - x}}S{e_x} + S{O_2}\left( {{\mathrm{unbalanced}}} \right)\end{equation}$$</annotation>

         </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202310256-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0db72383-4d95-40e2-a656-024c1ded5f58/adfm202310256-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0db72383-4d95-40e2-a656-024c1ded5f58/adfm202310256-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4f5491f6-2c36-4c47-ac9c-8cb913e65705/adfm202310256-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0db72383-4d95-40e2-a656-024c1ded5f58/adfm202310256-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202310256-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202310256" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphology and structural characterization of CoS<sub>2</sub>-<sub>x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG. a) Schematic illustration of the formation process of CoS<sub>2</sub>-<sub>x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG. HAADF-STEM image b), TEM image c), FFT pattern d), and STEM-EDS elemental maps e) of CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG. XRD patterns f), Raman spectra g), and S 2p–Se 3p XPS spectra h) of CoS<sub>2</sub>-<sub>x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG with different Se contents (<i>x</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;0, 0.4, 1.0, 1.6, 2) were obtained by controlling the molar ratio of the sulfur to selenium. The atomic ratio of several CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub> samples is semi-quantified by the SEM-EDS measurements, as shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). For simplicity, the several CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub> samples are named as CoS<sub>2</sub>@SG, CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG, CoSSe@SG, CoS<sub>0.4</sub>Se<sub>1.6</sub>@SG, and CoSe<sub>2</sub>@SG in the subsequent sections based on the added S/Se molar ratio.</p>
         
         <p>Then we used filed-emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) to study the morphology and structure of the CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG samples. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a> (Supporting Information), the Co@CoO@rGO consists of nanoparticles (30–50&nbsp;nm) anchoring on the graphene nanosheets. After synchronous selenization/sulfurization, the morphologies of the CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG composites show no significant change (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the stable CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG upon the synchronous selenization/sulfurization processes due to the strong chemical interaction between CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub> and SG. Then the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG was taken as an example to explore the detailed structure. The high-angle annular dark-field scanning TEM (HAADF-STEM, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0001">1b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a</a>, Supporting Information) and bright-field TEM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0001">1c</a>) images demonstrate that the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub> nanocrystals are firmly anchored on the graphene nanosheets. The size of the nanocrystals is ≈30–50&nbsp;nm. The nanocrystal-on-graphene structure effectively suppresses the nanocrystal agglomeration and concurrently offers a continuous network for high electronic conductivity. The fast Fourier-transform (FFT) pattern focused on a single CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub> nanocrystal shows a well-defined spotted pattern, demonstrating the single-crystal structure of the nanocrystal with diffractions along the [01-1] zone axis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0001">1d</a>). The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image of CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG exhibits well-resolved lattice fringes with an interplanar spacing of 0.279&nbsp;nm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4b</a>, Supporting Information), assigning to the (200) plane of CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>. This interplanar spacing is slightly larger than the (200) plane of standard pyrite CoS<sub>2</sub> (<i>D</i><sub>200</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;0.277&nbsp;nm, JCPDS No. 41–1471), indicating that the Se substitution in CoS<sub>2</sub> induces slight lattice expansion because of the larger ionic radius of Se<sup>2−</sup> (1.98&nbsp;Å) than S<sup>2−</sup> (1.84&nbsp;Å).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0025_R_d95533106e909" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Nano">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The STEM energy dispersive spectroscopy (STEM-EDS) mapping images show the uniform distribution of Co, S, and Se elements on the nanocrystal, demonstrating the successful Se substitution in CoS<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0001">1e</a>). Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) of CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG is carried out under air atmosphere (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), revealing the SG content in CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG is ≈16.9&nbsp;wt.%.</p>
         
         <p>The detailed crystal structures of CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG with different Se contents were characterized in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0001">1f–h</a>. As shown in the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0001">1f</a>), all the samples are highly crystalline and free of impurities. As for CoS<sub>2</sub>@SG, all the patterns can be indexed to cubic CoS<sub>2</sub> with <i>Pa-3</i> space group (JCPDS No. 00-41-1471). When the Se atoms substitution content is 0.4, the diffraction peaks of CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG shift toward lower angles due to unit cell expansion. This result proves that the Se substitution of S occurs at the atomic level,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0026_R_d95533106e955" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Catal.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is consistent with the TEM results. With the Se substitution content gradually increasing, the diffraction peaks gradually shift to lower angles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0001">1f</a>). Typically, with a complete Se substitution (CoSe<sub>2</sub>@SG), the diffraction peaks can be indexed to the cubic CoSe<sub>2</sub> with space group of <i>Pa</i>-3 (JCPDS No. 00-09-0234). Notably, the volume of the unit cell gradually increases with the continuous Se substitution (CoS<sub>2</sub>→CoSe<sub>2</sub>: 169.81→201.11&nbsp;Å<sup>3</sup>), which is beneficial for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. The structural evolution of CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG at different amounts of Se substitution was further revealed by Raman spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0001">1g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). The two distinct peaks at 1343 and 1583&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> belong to the D and G bands of reduced graphene oxide, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0027_R_d95533106e992" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ChemSusChem">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks at 254 and 382&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> correspond to the <i>E</i><sub>g</sub>, <i>A</i><sub>g</sub> modes of CoS<sub>2</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0028_R_d95533106e1008" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nat. Commun.">28</a>, <a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0029_R_d95533106e1008" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Eng. J.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While the peaks at 175, 461, 507, and 665&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are assigned to the <i>A</i><sub>g</sub>, <i>E</i><sub>g</sub>, <i>F</i><sub>2g</sub>, and <i>A</i><sub>1g</sub> modes of CoSe<sub>2</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0030_R_d95533106e1031" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Sci.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When the Se content is x&nbsp;=&nbsp;0.4, the <i>A</i><sub>g</sub> mode of CoS<sub>2</sub> splits into two peaks, which may stem from the difference in the structural arrangement of S/Se atoms bond to Co atoms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0031_R_d95533106e1042" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31">31</a>, <a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0032_R_d95533106e1042" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Appl. Phys.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When the Se content is <i>x</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1, the Raman modes of CoS<sub>2</sub> further shift to 241 and 313&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> due to the atomic rearrangement and structural strain, which partially alters crystal parameters.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0033_R_d95533106e1054" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Chem. Eng. J.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the <i>A</i><sub>1g</sub> mode of CoSe<sub>2</sub> starts to appear. Further increasing the Se content (<i>x</i>&nbsp;&gt;&nbsp;1), the Raman modes of CoS<sub>2</sub> tend to disappear, and those of CoSe<sub>2</sub> become more pronounced.</p>
         
         <p>Then we conducted the X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) for CoS<sub>2</sub>@SG, CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG, and CoSe<sub>2</sub>@SG. The survey XPS spectrum of CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG identifies the presence of Co, Se, S, and C elements in the composite (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a</a>, Supporting Information). The high-resolution Co 2p spectrum of CoS<sub>2</sub>@SG exhibits two peaks at 779.1 and 794.2&nbsp;eV (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7b</a>, Supporting Information), corresponding to the Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub> doublet, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0027_R_d95533106e1101" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ChemSusChem">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After Se substitution (<i>x</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;0.4, 1, 1.6, and 2), the Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> gradually shifts to lower binding energy (779.1&nbsp;eV→778.8&nbsp;eV), originating from the decrease of the oxidation state of Co due to the bonding of Co atoms with less electronegative Se atoms. This result indicates that the Se substitution could tune the d-band electronic structure of Co atoms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0025_R_d95533106e1110" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Nano">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As for the high-resolution S 2p spectrum of CoS<sub>2</sub>@SG (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0001">1h</a>), the two distinct peaks at 163.1&nbsp;eV and 164.3&nbsp;eV belong to S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of CoS<sub>2</sub>, respectively, while the doublet peaks at 165.2 and 166.4&nbsp;eV are ascribed to the C─S bond.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0034_R_d95533106e1128" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The existence of the C─S bond is strong evidence of S-doping into SG and strong chemical bonding between CoS<sub>2</sub> and SG nanosheets. The presence of Co─S─C bond is confirmed by the C─S ratio. Based on the high-resolution S 2p spectra, the C─S ratio among all the S atoms in the CoS<sub>2</sub>@SG is ≈17.5%. Based on the survey spectrum, the atomic ratio of S : Co is ≈2.35:1. The excess S should be the S dopant in graphene (C─S), accounting for a ratio of 14.9%. This value is lower than the whole C─S ratio (≈17.5%) obtained by S 2p spectra, indicating part of S in C─S bonds comes from CoS<sub>2</sub>. Thus, the Co─S─C bond exists in the CoS<sub>2</sub>@SG. After Se incorporation (<i>x</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;0.4, 1, and 1.6), the S 2p of CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG gradually shifts to lower binding energy, suggesting that the Se has been successfully doped into CoS<sub>2</sub> crystal and enriched the electron cloud around S atoms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0035_R_d95533106e1150" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Chem. Eng. J.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the doublet peaks representing the C─S bond still exist in the CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG sample, confirming the successful S-doping in SG. Besides, two additional weak peaks at 161.1&nbsp;and 166.0&nbsp;eV correspond to Se 3p<sub>3/2</sub> and Se 3p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0025_R_d95533106e1164" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Nano">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the Se substitution content increases, the signal from Se 3p becomes stronger. Notably, the Se 3d XPS spectra reveal that Se─C signal exists in the CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7c</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the Se substitution process can also induce the Se─C bond formation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0036_R_d95533106e1177" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The S, Se-codoping can be further confirmed by the low magnification STEM-EDS elemental maps of CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information), where clear S&amp;Se signals appear on the SG nanosheet region apart from the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub> nanoparticles. The robust C─S and C─Se bonds could provide a strong coupling between the SG matrix and CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub> and thus ensure long-term cycling stability.</p>
         
         <p>To evaluate the effect of different Se substitution ratios in CoS<sub>2</sub>, we investigated the sodium storage performance of the CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG in a voltage window of 0.4–3&nbsp;V. The mass loading of the electrodes is ≈0.85–1.2&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> (see details in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG show a series of weak peaks between 1.26 and 0.8&nbsp;V for the first cathode sweep (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a>, Supporting Information), which are mainly ascribed to the initial Na<sup>+</sup> insertion reaction (Na<sub>y</sub>CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>) and formation of stable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0037_R_d95533106e1229" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chem. Eng. J.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The strong peak at 0.61&nbsp;V can be attributed to the conversion reaction from sodiated CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub> to Co and Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se. During the first anodic scan, the three oxidation peaks at 1.75, 1.91, and 2.01&nbsp;V correspond to the multi-step desodiation processes. From the second scan onward, the first cathode peaks appear at 1.47&nbsp;V due to the electrochemical activation process and structural reorganization of the electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0035_R_d95533106e1243" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Chem. Eng. J.">35</a>, <a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0038_R_d95533106e1243" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Nano">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) profiles of the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG electrode deliver the initial charge/discharge capacities of 572.2/599.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to an ultrahigh initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 95.5% (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0002">2a</a>). Moreover, the CE reaches ≈100% in the second cycle and remains stable in subsequent cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a>, Supporting Information). In the case of other CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG composites, they also show high and stable CEs (≈100%) since the second cycle (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9c</a>, Supporting Information). The above results reveal that ultrathin and robust SEI layers form on the electrode surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0039_R_d95533106e1272" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Angew. Chem.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The ultrathin and robust SEI layer was confirmed by the TEM measurement based on the cycled CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). At the 1st cycle, an ultrathin and uniform layer (≈3.5&nbsp;nm) adheres to the electrode surface (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a>, Supporting Information), corresponding to the SEI layer. After 12 cycles, the thickness of SEI remains at ≈3.4&nbsp;nm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a>, Supporting Information), demonstrating the great structural stability of the SEI upon cycling. The stable CE also suggests that the CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub> nanocrystals undergo small volume change and negligible nanocrystal crack, which could prevent continuous SEI fracture and growth. The cycling performance reveals that the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG electrode displays the highest reversible capacity of 507.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles, higher than the other CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0002">2b</a>). Notably, the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@SG delivers a much lower capacity (325.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) after 100 cycles, indicating that a large amount of Se substitution greatly reduces the Na<sup>+</sup> storage active sites. Based on the theoretical capacity (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) and electrochemical testing, the increase of Se substitution would decrease the specific capacity of the CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG electrode. The rate capability of different CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG electrodes was then carried out under different current densities from 0.5 to 30&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0002">2c</a>). The CoS<sub>2</sub>@SG electrode shows a high capacity (≈563&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) at a low current density of 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, but the lowest capacity retention (≈45.3%) at 30&nbsp;A g<sup>−1</sup>, suggesting the poor Na<sup>+</sup> reaction kinetics. The CoSe<sub>2</sub>@SG electrode displays the lowest capacity (≈413&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, but the highest capacity retention (≈76%) at 30&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. In contrast, the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG displays the best rate performance with a high capacity of 576.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 411.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 30.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (≈71.4% capacity retention), demonstrating an optimized Se substitution. In addition, the GCD curves of CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information) show a small voltage difference as the current density rises, further confirming the excellent reaction kinetics of the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG electrode. To the best of our knowledge, the rate capability of CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG is the best among all the CoS<sub>2</sub>-based materials for SIBs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0002">2d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0037_R_d95533106e1387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chem. Eng. J.">37</a>, <a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0038_R_d95533106e1387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Nano">38</a>, <a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0040_R_d95533106e1387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nanoscale">40</a>-<a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0046_R_d95533106e1387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Chem. Eng. J.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Then we compared the pseudocapacitive contributions of the CoS<sub>2</sub>@SG and CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG electrodes based on the CV curves at various scan rates (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). Through calculation, the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG electrode exhibits a large pseudocapacitive contribution of 98.1% at 1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, which is higher than the CoS<sub>2</sub>@SG electrode (≈97.3%) (see details in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). The high pseudocapacitive contribution is beneficial for the excellent electrochemical kinetics and reversibility, which ensures high rate capability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202310256-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a66d349a-6da3-4dc1-b601-4a653db0a3b5/adfm202310256-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a66d349a-6da3-4dc1-b601-4a653db0a3b5/adfm202310256-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/82d905fc-cebc-45fc-bf79-9888168782e8/adfm202310256-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a66d349a-6da3-4dc1-b601-4a653db0a3b5/adfm202310256-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202310256-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202310256" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of CoS<sub>2</sub><sub>-</sub><sub>x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG. a) Discharge and charge profiles of CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG electrode at different cycles. b) Cycling performance at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Rate capabilities at various current densities. d) Rate comparison with CoS<sub>2</sub>-based works. e) EIS plots at 10th cycles. f) GITT profiles at pulse current of 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. g) Long-term cycling performance at 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To better explore the electrochemical behaviors of the CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG electrodes, kinetic studies based on electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) were carried out. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0002">2e</a>, all the EIS spectra at the 10th cycle show a semicircle at high frequency and an inclined line at low frequency, corresponding to the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) and Warburg impedance (<i>R</i><sub>w</sub>), respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0047_R_d95533106e1469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The fitting results of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> are shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG and CoSe<sub>2</sub>@SG is much lower than the CoS<sub>2</sub>@SG electrode (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0003">3a</a>), indicating the Se substitution can greatly enhance the interfacial reaction kinetics by improving the intrinsic electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0019_R_d95533106e1499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Z. Sun, Energy Storage Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the EIS spectra, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) of CoS<sub>2</sub>@SG, CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG, and CoSe<sub>2</sub>@SG are 8.1&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−13</sup>, 1.5&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−11</sup>, 2.4&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−11</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting that the Se substitution significantly promotes the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. The low interfacial resistance and enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion are beneficial for improving rate performance. After 20 cycles, the charge transfer resistance <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> remains unchanged (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13b</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the stable electrode/electrolyte interface. To further verify the enhanced reaction kinetics by Se substitution, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) test was carried out in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0002">2f</a>, and the detailed calculation of <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information). As revealed, the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG presents the highest capacity and higher <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> than the CoS<sub>2</sub>@SG, whereas the CoSe<sub>2</sub>@SG shows the highest <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> but lowest capacity during the sodiation/desodiation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0002">2f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). These results are consistent with the electrochemical performance, revealing the importance of rational Se substitution (<i>x</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;0.4).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202310256-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3c8729a4-51e3-4cbe-ba47-a1a30b280ac4/adfm202310256-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3c8729a4-51e3-4cbe-ba47-a1a30b280ac4/adfm202310256-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8564d128-2a59-4f02-95dd-12fb9a46239b/adfm202310256-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3c8729a4-51e3-4cbe-ba47-a1a30b280ac4/adfm202310256-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202310256-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202310256" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Reaction mechanism of CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG. a) Initial GCD of the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG. b) In situ XRD contour map. c) In situ XRD patterns with selected cycles. d–g) TEM image, HRTEM images, SAED pattern, and STEM-EDS elemental mappings at fully sodiated state, and h–k) TEM image, HRTEM images, FFT pattern, and STEM-EDS elemental mappings at fully desodiated state.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The long-term cycling performance of several CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG electrodes was further compared at a high current density of 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0002">2g</a>, all the samples show stable cycling over 1000 cycles, indicating the ultra-stable structure of CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG enabled by the nanocrystal-on-graphene configuration and robust C─S and C─Se bonds. Moreover, the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG electrode delivers a high capacity of 542.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles, holding an ultrahigh capacity retention of 97.6%. In addition, at a higher mass loading (≈3.65&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>), the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG electrode delivers high capacities at relatively lower current densities (≤2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), comparable to that at a mass loading of 1.06&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). However, at relatively higher current densities (≥5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), the capacities rapidly decrease due to the enlarged Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion length of the thick electrode. Overall, the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG electrode exhibits the best sodium storage performance in comparison to other CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG, indicating that the optimal Se substitution (<i>x</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;0.4) could concurrently realize enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics and Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity.</p>
         
         <p>To reveal the reaction mechanism of CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG composite, in situ<i>/</i>ex situ measurements were conducted upon the sodiation/desodiation processes (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4</a>). In situ XRD was measured at a current density of 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4a</a>), during which a series of XRD patterns were collected (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4b</a>). The strong peaks at 38.6°, 41.2°, 43.9°, and 45.9° are ascribed to the Be and BeO. As shown in the contour map, the pristine CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG shows four prominent peaks at 27.7°, 32.1°, 36.0°, and 39.5°, corresponding to the (111), (200), (210), and (211) planes of the cubic CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub> with space group of <i>Pa-3</i>. As the sodiation proceeds (from open circuit voltage to ≈1.1&nbsp;V, stage I), the four peaks gradually shift to lower diffraction angles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4b,c</a>), indicating the crystal structure expansion induced by Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub> (Equation 4).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0048_R_d95533106e1710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Energy Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At stage II, the angles of the four peaks remain unchanged, but the intensity gradually becomes weak, which implies the conversion reaction between Na<sub>x</sub>CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub> and Na<sup>+</sup> (Equation 5). The conversion reaction is further confirmed by the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>S &amp; Na<sub>2</sub>Se at the end of sodiation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4b</a>), where the broad peak ≈37.5–39.0° corresponds to the (220) plane of Na<sub>2</sub>S &amp; Na<sub>2</sub>Se and peaks at 44.2° and 46.1° belong to Na<sub>2</sub>Se (311) and Na<sub>2</sub>S (311), respectively. However, no obvious peaks associated with Co metals can be observed, possibly due to the ultrasmall crystal size of the resultant Co nanograins.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0048_R_d95533106e1740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Energy Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon the desodiation process (stage III), the peak of Na<sub>2</sub>S and Na<sub>2</sub>Se gradually becomes weak and almost disappears at the end of charge (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4b,c</a>), suggesting the conversion reaction between Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se and Co<sup>0</sup> and possible formation of CoS<sub>2</sub> and CoSe<sub>2</sub> (Equation 6). However, the peaks of CoS<sub>2</sub> and CoSe<sub>2</sub> could not be detected at a fully charged state, implying the poor crystallinity nature of the product upon desodiation. The undetectable Co metals and CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoSe<sub>2</sub> will be detected and analyzed later by ex situ TEM measurement. The relevant redox reactions are as follows:</p>

            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202310256-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2b616e17-57f6-4e19-a51a-ae5305d9b1ef/adfm202310256-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>

         </section>

                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                                    <mn>4</mn>
                                    <mi>N</mi>
                                    <msup>
                                       <mi>a</mi>
                                       <mo>+</mo>
                                    </msup>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                                    <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                                    <mn>4</mn>
                                    <msup>
                                       <mi>e</mi>
                                       <mo>−</mo>
                                    </msup>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mtd>
                           </mtr>
                        </mtable>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{eqnarray}&amp;&amp;1.6N{a_2}S + 0.4N{a_2}Se + Co \to 0.8Co{S_2}\nonumber\\ &amp;&amp; + 0.2CoS{e_2} + 4N{a^ + } + {\mathrm{\;}}4{e^ - }\end{eqnarray}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(6)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>Ex situ TEM measurements were performed further to study the reaction mechanism and structural evolution of CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0001">1c</a>, the pristine CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub> consists of nanoparticles with sizes ≈30–50&nbsp;nm. At a full sodiation state of 0.4&nbsp;V, the nanoparticles slightly expand to 40–60&nbsp;nm due to the Na<sup>+</sup> uptake (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17a,b</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the nanoparticles become highly porous due to the conversion reaction that induces phase separation between Co<sup>0</sup> and Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0049_R_d95533106e2024" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in the high-resolution TEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4e</a>), the Co<sup>0</sup> nanocrystals with a size of 9–13&nbsp;nm are separately encapsulated by the Na<sub>2</sub>S and Na<sub>2</sub>Se domains. The encapsulation of Co<sup>0</sup> nanocrystals by the Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se domain prevents the Co° coarsening and ensures high reaction kinetics between Co<sup>0</sup> and Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se. The formation of Co<sup>0</sup> and Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se also confirms the conversion reaction of CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>. The corresponding selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern shows a series of diffraction rings, corresponding to the hexagonal Co<sup>0</sup> (JCPDS No. 01–1278), cubic Na<sub>2</sub>S (JCPDS No. 47–1698), and Na<sub>2</sub>Se (JCPDS No.47–1699), respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4f</a>). The energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) element mapping measurement under STEM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4g</a>) further confirms the porous structure of the discharged CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub> and shows uniform distribution of Co, S, and Se elements over the porous nanoparticles. After full desodiation (2.5&nbsp;V), the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub> nanoparticles slightly recover to 30–50&nbsp;nm due to the Na<sup>+</sup> removal (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4h</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17c,d</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub> nanoparticles become nearly nonporous due to the conversion reaction between Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se and Co<sup>0</sup> nanocrystals. The great “poreforming-repairing” phenomenon at the nanoscale demonstrates the stable nanocrystal-on-graphene structure with C─S─Co and C─Se─Co bonding, which prevents the nanocrystal self-fracture and electron disconnection upon cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0050_R_d95533106e2103" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Energy Storage Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The HRTEM images display clear lattice fringes with interplanar distances of 0.17, 0.24, and 0.195&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4i</a>), corresponding to the (311) and (211) planes of cubic CoSe<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS No.09-0234), and (220) plane of cubic CoS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS No. 41–1471). Moreover, the CoSe<sub>2</sub> domain closely connects with the CoS<sub>2</sub> domain, forming the CoSe<sub>2</sub>–CoS<sub>2</sub> heterostructure. The corresponding FFT pattern shows two sets of diffraction rings, well indexed to the cubic CoSe<sub>2</sub> and CoS<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4j</a>). The STEM-EDS elemental maps display the uniform distribution of Co, S, and Se elements over the charged sample (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4k</a>). The HRTEM images and FFT pattern reveal that the conversion reaction between Co<sup>0</sup> and Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se in situ generates the CoSe<sub>2</sub>–CoS<sub>2</sub> heterostructure instead of the pristine CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub> through an electrochemical self-driven process. The CoSe<sub>2</sub>–CoS<sub>2</sub> heterostructure will be reversibly cycled in the subsequent cycles, as confirmed by the ex situ TEM&amp;HRTEM images after 5 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). The formation of micro-scaled CoSe<sub>2</sub>–CoS<sub>2</sub> heterostructure can fully take advantage of the high-capacity CoS<sub>2</sub> and kinetically favorable CoSe<sub>2</sub>. Moreover, the CoSe<sub>2</sub>–CoS<sub>2</sub> heterostructures with rich phase boundaries could facilitate the Na<sup>+</sup> migration and increase the electrochemical reactivity,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0051_R_d95533106e2173" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">51</a>-<a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0053_R_d95533106e2173" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Nano Lett.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> leading to high sodium storage capacity and enhanced reaction kinetics. Thus, the balanced charge storage and reaction kinetics result in the optimal performance of the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG.</p>
         
         <p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were then conducted to understand the critical role of Se substitution in CoS<sub>2</sub>. We selected 2&nbsp;×&nbsp;2 supercell of CoS<sub>2</sub> (200) surface containing 32 Co and 56 S atoms as the pristine model, then we built three models based on the element ratio, and the optimized Na adsorption structural models are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4a</a>. As revealed, the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub> exhibits a Na adsorption energy of −3.29&nbsp;eV on the (200) surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4b</a>), lower than the CoS<sub>2</sub> (−2.73&nbsp;eV), suggesting the Se substitution can effectively enhance the interaction between Na atom and CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub> and thus benefit the Na uptake.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0018_R_d95533106e2207" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As for the CoSe<sub>2</sub> with complete Se substitution, the Na adsorption energy further reduces to −3.97&nbsp;eV, demonstrating a strong Na capture ability. Then the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier was calculated by the nudged elastic band method, where two optimal diffusion paths based on the (200) surface are used, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4c,e</a>. In path one, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier is 0.24&nbsp;eV for CoS<sub>2</sub>, 0.12&nbsp;eV for CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>, and 0.05&nbsp;eV for CoSe<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4d</a>). The results theoretically verify the enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics by the Se implantation, which agrees well with the experimental kinetic analysis. In contrast, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier is 0.22&nbsp;eV for CoS<sub>2</sub>, 0.14&nbsp;eV for CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>, and 0.145&nbsp;eV for CoSe<sub>2</sub> in path two (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0004">4f</a>). Although the CoSe<sub>2</sub> shows a similar energy barrier to CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>, both of them deliver a lower energy barrier than the CoS<sub>2</sub>, further confirming Se substitution can enhance the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. Moreover, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier in CoSe<sub>2</sub> is significantly lower in path one, suggesting that Na<sup>+</sup> is more likely to migrate along path one. Notably, when the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub> was in situ transformed into CoSe<sub>2</sub>–CoS<sub>2</sub> heterostructure, the minor CoSe<sub>2</sub> phase acts as a Na<sup>+</sup> “trapper” to enable the fast Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption on the heterostructure as well as an ionic conductor for fast Na<sup>+</sup> conduction during the (de)sodiation process, finally contributing to enhanced ionic transfer and reaction reversibility of the CoSe<sub>2</sub>–CoS<sub>2</sub> heterostructure. On the other hand, the major CoS<sub>2</sub> phase contributes a higher Na-storage capacity to the heterostructure. The synergic effect of CoSe<sub>2</sub> and CoS<sub>2</sub> results in the optimal electrochemical performance of CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG.</p>
         
         <p>A sodium-ion full battery is then assembled by using the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C cathode (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0005">5a</a>). The half-cell performances of the anode and cathode are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0005">5b</a>. Then the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C full cell was tested within the voltage window of 1.0–3.5&nbsp;V. All the capacities are obtained based on the anode mass to evaluate the ability of anode. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0005">5c</a>, the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C full cell delivers a high capacity of 425&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 1.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The average capacities of the full cell at 1.1, 2.2, 4.4, 5.5, 11.0, and 27.6&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> are 422.4, 362.3, 327.5, 315.7, 280.9, and 211.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310256-fig-0005">5b,c</a>), suggesting the excellent rate performance due to the enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics in the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG electrode. Furthermore, the long-term cycling performance at 5.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (after rate performance) exhibits a high capacity of 295&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles, showing a high capacity retention ratio of 95.2%. The full cell performance suggests that the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG is a promising anode material for practical SIBs application. Meanwhile, the “SIB” sign composed of 53 light-emitting diodes (LEDs) can be lighten up by one coin cell, further confirming the potential practical applications of this device. Notably, the potential of the CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG in half-cell (1.5–1.7&nbsp;V) is relatively higher than the hard carbon anode, which will decrease the energy density of the full cell. However, the higher specific capacity and excellent rate capability of CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG can mitigate this drawback to some extent. Moreover, the low potential of hard carbon may be vulnerable to the plating of sodium metal and further formation of sodium dendrites, showing the risk of safety issues.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310256-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310256-bib-0054_R_d95533106e2390" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Environ Sci">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the higher potential of CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG can avoid this issue.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202310256-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7705a4d8-8c23-4cfb-aee9-faac8d692136/adfm202310256-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7705a4d8-8c23-4cfb-aee9-faac8d692136/adfm202310256-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b12e527e-bbe1-47ef-b3fc-5043a90326ed/adfm202310256-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7705a4d8-8c23-4cfb-aee9-faac8d692136/adfm202310256-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cells. a) Schematic illustration of the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C full cell. b) Charge/discharge profiles of the Na||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C and Na||CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG half cells. c) Charge/discharge profiles of the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C full cell. d) Rate capability at various current densities. e) Long-term cycling performance at 5.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Inset is the digital photographs of the “SIB” composing of 53 LEDs.</div>
               </figcaption>

         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202310256-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202310256-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have developed a series of Se substituted CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG composite through a synchronous selenization/sulfurization approach. The selenization/sulfurization process also induces strong C─S and C─Se bonding between CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub> and SG, greatly improving the structural stability of the CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub>@SG. Thus, the CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub> nanocrystals experience a reversible “poreforming−repairing” behavior during the sodiation/desodiation process, effectively buffering the CoS<sub>2-x</sub>Se<sub>x</sub> nanocrystals from agglomeration and local electron disconnection. Moreover, the Se anion substitution significantly promotes the Na<sup>+</sup> reaction kinetics, while excess Se substitution reduces the Na<sup>+</sup> storage active sites, as evidenced by electrochemical measurements, kinetics analysis, and theoretical calculation. In situ XRD and ex situ TEM reveal that the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG undergoes a conversion reaction mechanism and generates a CoSe<sub>2</sub>–CoS<sub>2</sub> heterostructure upon cycling, which fully takes advantage of the high-capacity CoS<sub>2</sub> and kinetically favorable CoSe<sub>2</sub>. Due to the double-edged electrochemical behavior and nano/micro-scaled structure optimization, the CoS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub>@SG delivers the best sodium storage performance: ultralong cycle life with 97.6% capacity retention after 1000 cycles, ultrahigh rate capability with a high capacity of 412.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 30&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Our work provides a controllable Se substitution approach in TMSs and reveals the electrochemical mechanisms of the substitution systems, which affords great opportunities for exploring superior SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
